A Fuzzy Bayesian Analysis of Deductive Argumentation
26 Jan 2022

1
Now that Iâve finally gotten around to writing out some of the more fundamental components of my epistemology,1 I can at last proceed to writing one of the articles that Iâve been planning to do pretty much since I started writing on this blog months ago: a fuzzy Bayesian analysis of deductive philosophical argumentation. This was inspired in part by a discussion of how arguments work on the part of Dr. Graham Oppy. In that interview, he discusses how deductive arguments in philosophy are actually far less effective than they are typically supposed to be, at least in more classically-inclined circles (e.g. philosophy of religion). When I heard1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThank you for providing additional context. It seems the focus here is a specific application of Fuzzy Bayesian Analysis to the evaluation of deductive philosophical argumentation, inspired by Dr. Graham Oppy's insights on the effectiveness of such arguments.Here's how this additional information connects to the previous topics:Fuzzy Bayesian Analysis of Deductive Argumentation: The fusion of fuzzy logic with Bayesian analysis in philosophy mirrors the Fuzzy Bayesian Learning method. Both approaches aim to represent and analyze uncertainty, combining fuzzy sets (capturing vagueness) and Bayesian inference (updating probabilities based on data). In the context of deductive argumentation, this could provide a more nuanced understanding of how arguments work, considering the vagueness and uncertainty inherent in philosophical concepts.St. Petersburg Paradox and Bernoulli's Fallacy: These can be seen as instances where traditional mathematical reasoning fails to capture human intuition. Similarly, Dr. Oppy's comments suggest that traditional deductive arguments in philosophy might not always be effective. Fuzzy Bayesian analysis could offer a tool to reconcile mathematical analysis with human reasoning in both contexts.Frequentist vs. Bayesian Approaches: The use of Bayesian methods in this context continues to emphasize the flexibility of Bayesian thinking in various fields, including philosophy.Applying to Philosophy of Religion and Other Areas: The specific mention of classical philosophical fields, such as the philosophy of religion, shows how statistical methodologies like Fuzzy Bayesian Analysis can be applied even in disciplines traditionally far removed from mathematical and statistical thinking.Critical Evaluation of Deductive Reasoning: The blog seems to be critical of the effectiveness of traditional deductive arguments in philosophy, perhaps implying that they are too rigid or fail to capture the complexities of philosophical thinking. This critique aligns with the emphasis on incorporating human-like reasoning and complexity into mathematical models, like Fuzzy Bayesian Learning.In summary, the shared theme across these connections is the application of complex statistical methodologies, specifically those that blend fuzzy logic and Bayesian inference, to fields where uncertainty, vagueness, and human-like reasoning are key. This includes philosophical argumentation, decision-making processes, and possibly areas like finance or health, where Fuzzy Bayesian Learning could also be applied.1 / 1Before I move on to the next phase of this essay, let me briefly discuss a few other miscellaneous notes which, while probably intuitively obvious, still deserve to be stated explicitly.

First of all, when a premise is actually the combination of multiple propositions through various conjunctions, the probability of the premise being true is constituted by the individual probabilities of each of the propositions which compose the premise, combined according to the statistical rules for each of the conjunctions involved.

Second, following on from points I made in my first essay on fuzzy Bayesian logic, assigning specific probabilities to propositions and arguments is a very dangerous game, more likely to lead to nonsense and the appearance of certainty than it is to actually get you closer to the truth. The point of these mathematical equations is more to demonstrate general rules of thumb and approaches from combining or relating the fuzzy, intuitive confidence levels you have in your head. I only specify specific values for things to make the examples a bit easier to understand, but you have to realize that theyâre just toy examples with random values.

4
One thing you might have noticed while I was going through the examples above is that, on the whole, deductive arguments just arenât that effective. After all, if you need all of the premises of your argument to be true simultaneously for the conclusion to be true, thatâs a really big burden; the probability that youâll get what you want given that we donât live in a world of absolute certainty scales inversely geometrically with the number of premises you have. This is why science, which increases statistical credences in hypothesis by way of going out and checking them directly, where the only possible error is the likelihood of getting a result that makes it look like your hypothesis is true without it actually being true, is so much more effective than armchair logic. Instead of building a conclusion on a house of cards that could fail if even a single premise turns out to be wrong, when all of them have a non-insignificant chance of being wrong, it just skips directly to demonstrating whether the final proposition you are interested in proving or disproving is a useful way to look at the world.

This is also why arguments that have a lot of premises, while they look impressive, are actually incredibly dubious and weak. After all, the more premises you have, the more certain you have to be in all your premises to have even the bare minimum level of certainty in your conclusion. Take, for example, Plantingaâs modal ontological argument:

There is a possible world W in which there exists a being with maximal greatness.

Maximal greatness entails having maximal excellence in every possible world.

Maximal excellence entails omniscience, omnipotence, and moral perfection in every possible world.

So in W there exists a being which is omniscient, omnipotent, and morally perfect in every possible world.

So in W the proposition âThere is no omniscient, omnipotent, and morally perfect beingâ is impossible.

But what is impossible in one possible world is impossible in every possible world.

So the proposition âThere is no omniscient, omnipotent, and morally perfect beingâ is impossible in the actual world.

So there is in the actual world an omniscient, omnipotent, and morally perfect being.2

For the argument to justifiably even slightly budge someoneâs credence in the theory that a god exists up from being agnostic or even negative toward it, the soundness probability of the argument needs to be at least \(51%\). That means that the probabilities of each of the premises being sound need to all multiply up to at least \(51%\). We can estimate the average necessary soundness probability for each premise, therefore, by calculating the seventh root of \(0.51\), which is \(0.908\). This means that we would need to be on average 91% sure of each of these problematic and hotly contested, abstract, premises. And even that 0.51% would be a drop in the bucket compared to the inherent improbability of a maximally great being existing.

The inherent uncertainty of the premises of an argument, and the resulting weakness of philosophical arguments in general, is not actually the only problem with deductive philosophical arguments. Since the rules of logic themselves are constructed by human philosophers, in an attempt to describe what they think constitutes the rules of meaning and basic ontology, they themselves are not strictly certain, and so some logical rules, which are not directly entailed by the criteria for meaning I covered in my previous epistemology article, bring with them their own kind of uncertainty. For instance, the validity of the S5 axiom of modal logic when it pertains to metaphysical truth is hotly contested in some circles, and so the use of this axiom should be considered as something that decreases the likelihood that an argument is valid. Furthermore, the specific way that you actually run logical rules on your premises may even be incorrect, and the likelihood of this also increases with the size and complexity of your argument. All of this is why if youâre going to criticize an argument, you donât actually have to point to one of the premises, you can instead call out problems and equivocations that happen in-between the premises and the conclusion. All of these factors must also be integrated into the probability that an argument actually demonstrates its premise, turning the âsoundness probabilityâ Iâve been talking about so far into a âdemonstration probabilityâ that includes both soundness and validity.

4
This indicates a core problem with philosophical argumentation as it has been done in the past, since at least Aristotle. Simply sitting back in your armchair and coming up with a list of premises and assumptions that seem likely to you, based on your own intuitions, and then stringing them together into long streams of argumentation is just not effective enough when you actually treat the credence levels you should have in your premises with honesty and humility. What, then, should we do? Should philosophers retreat to conceptual analysis and clarification, philosophy of science, math, and logic, synthesizing the conclusions of science together, and documenting and understanding properties of the human condition (whether in the manner of phenomenologists and epistemologists or existentialists)? While these are all worthy goals, and are actually the fields that I am most interested in and in which I think the discipline of philosophy has the most to offer, there is nevertheless a place for philosophy in cosmology and metaphysics too â it is just that a different route must be taken to ensure it remains useful in those areas.

This is where Graham Oppyâs views on the nature of philosophical argumentation come in. Instead of focusing on arguments for particular conclusions, which are necessarily piecemeal and therefore fall afoul of the geometric decrease in probability as the number of premises grow, one should consider holistic theories concerning some subject, and compare the explanatory and predictive power of the theory, as well as the intellectual virtues the theory demonstrates as a whole, with the intellectual âcostâ of the theory. This approach relies primarily on the ability to state a set of core premises and then use conceptual analysis to elaborate on their implications, in order to derive specific hypothesis and sub-theories, instead of oneâs ability to construct deductive philosophical arguments of the kind spoken about above. Moreover, it directly maps the process of choosing beliefs concerning some phenomena onto the underlying pragmatic reasons that we choose beliefs, instead of being based on logic, which is in turn based on those pragmatic reasons, and therefore only being based on pragmatic reasons by proxy. This also allows you to apply the fuzzy Bayesian logic described in my previous articles more directly and conveniently to the theories themselves.

In conclusion, then, applying my fuzzy Bayesian reasoning to theories of certain phenomena as a whole, according to their intellectual virtues and costs, and relying on science to demonstrate the predictive power of such theories, is by far superior to relying on deductive philosophical argument, due to the inherent nature of how uncertainty interacts with deductive arguments1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe lengthy excerpt you've provided offers a compelling critique of traditional deductive philosophical argumentation, particularly emphasizing the limitations and pitfalls of relying on long chains of premises and deductive logic. Here's how the information in the text relates to the concepts discussed earlier:Combination of Multiple Propositions: The text explains that the probability of a premise, constituted by multiple propositions, must be calculated according to statistical rules. Fuzzy Rule-Based Bayesian Reasoning Approach for Prioritization of Failures in FMEA
October 2008IEEE Transactions on Reliability 57(3):517 - 528
DOI:10.1109/TR.2008.928208
SourceIEEE Xplore
Authors:
Zaili Yang
Liverpool John Moores University
Stephen Bonsall
Liverpool John Moores University

Request full-text

To read the full-text of this research, you can request a copy directly from the authors.

Abstract
This paper presents a novel, efficient fuzzy rule-based Bayesian reasoning ( FuRBaR ) approach for prioritizing failures in failure mode and effects analysis ( FMEA ). The technique is specifically intended to deal with some of the drawbacks concerning the use of conventional fuzzy logic (i.e. rule-based) methods in FMEA . In the proposed approach, subjective belief degrees are assigned to the consequent part of the rules to model the incompleteness encountered in establishing the knowledge base. A Bayesian reasoning mechanism is then used to aggregate all relevant rules for assessing and prioritizing potential failure modes. A series of case studies of collision risk between a floating, production, storage, and off loading ( FPSO ) system and a shuttle tanker caused by technical failure during tandem off loading operation is used to illustrate the application of the proposed model. The reliability of the new approach is tested by using a benchmarking technique (with a well-established fuzzy rule-based evidential reasoning method), and a sensitivity analysis of failure priority values.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe abstract you provided refers to a research paper that introduces a novel method called "Fuzzy Rule-Based Bayesian Reasoning" (FuRBaR) for prioritizing failures in Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (FMEA). Here's a summary of the key aspects and how they relate to the topic you were discussing earlier:Novel Approach (FuRBaR): The paper presents a new method that combines fuzzy rule-based systems with Bayesian reasoning. This combination aims to tackle specific challenges in the field of FMEA.Addressing Drawbacks in Conventional Methods: Traditional fuzzy logic methods in FMEA have limitations. The proposed approach seeks to rectify these by modeling the incompleteness encountered in establishing the knowledge base.Use of Subjective Belief Degrees: The system allows for the inclusion of subjective belief degrees within the rules, allowing for a more nuanced and flexible understanding of uncertainty, which aligns with the themes of uncertainty and complexity discussed earlier.Bayesian Reasoning for Aggregation: By employing Bayesian reasoning, the system can aggregate various rules to assess and prioritize potential failure modes. This offers a more dynamic and probabilistic way of understanding risk, aligning with the prior discussion of Bayesian methods in philosophy.Real-World Application and Testing: The model's applicability is demonstrated through case studies related to collision risks between floating systems and tankers. Its reliability is further validated through benchmarking and sensitivity analysis.Connection to Prior Discussion: The method discussed in this abstract continues the theme of integrating fuzzy logic with Bayesian reasoning to handle complex, uncertain scenarios. What is fuzzy Bayesian logic?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCertainly! Fuzzy Bayesian Logic is a fusion of fuzzy logic and Bayesian reasoning, and it was touched upon in our conversation through the exploration of Fuzzy Bayesian Reasoning (FBR) models.Fuzzy Logic: Fuzzy logic extends classical (Boolean) logic to handle uncertainty and imprecision. It does this by allowing values to have degrees of membership in a set, rather than just "true" or "false." This can be very useful in modeling complex systems where information might be vague or incomplete.Bayesian Reasoning: Bayesian reasoning is a method of statistical inference in which Bayes' theorem is used to update the probability of a hypothesis as more evidence or information becomes available. It's a way of incorporating prior knowledge and observed data to calculate the probability of a particular outcome or state.Fuzzy Bayesian Reasoning (FBR): Combining these two concepts, Fuzzy Bayesian Reasoning uses fuzzy logic to deal with uncertainty in the priors, likelihoods, or evidence within a Bayesian framework. It essentially incorporates the 'fuzziness' of information into Bayesian models, allowing for a more nuanced and flexible approach to statistical inference.In our conversation, we discussed examples of how Fuzzy Bayesian Reasoning was used in evaluating climate risks in the UK road transport networks and as an innovative decision-aiding tool in quantitative analysis.Fuzzy Bayesian Logic, therefore, represents a sophisticated approach to dealing with uncertainty, by blending the crisp probabilistic reasoning of Bayesian methods with the more nuanced, gradated approach of fuzzy logic. It can be particularly useful in complex systems where data may be imprecise, ambiguous, or incomplete.1 / 1Thank you.
